    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8027275635448979911">A story with no real beginning or end (it ends in the middle of a sentence and begins in the middle of the same sentence), this "book of Doublends Jined" is as remarkable for its prose as for its circular structure. <br/><br/>Written in a fantastic dream-language, forged from polyglot puns and portmanteau words, the Wake features some of Joyce's most hilarious characters: the Irish </span>
  <span id="freeText8027275635448979911" style="display:none">A story with no real beginning or end (it ends in the middle of a sentence and begins in the middle of the same sentence), this "book of Doublends Jined" is as remarkable for its prose as for its circular structure. <br /><br />Written in a fantastic dream-language, forged from polyglot puns and portmanteau words, the Wake features some of Joyce's most hilarious characters: the Irish barkeep Humphrey Chimpden Earwicker, Shem the Penman, Shaun the Postman, and Anna Livia Plurabelle. <br /><br />Joyce's final work, Finnegan's Wake is his masterpiece of the night as Ulysses is of the day. Supreme linguistic virtuosity conjures up the dark underground worlds of sexuality and dream. Joyce undermines traditional storytelling and all official forms of English and confronts the different kinds of betrayal - cultural, political and sexual - that he saw at the heart of Irish history. Dazzlingly inventive, with passages of great lyrical beauty and humour, Finnegans Wake remains one of the most remarkable works of the twentieth century.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8027275635448979911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I found it funny and dense and horribly difficult to get. All at the same time. It was fun, I didn't read all of it, but wherever I opened the page, I<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I found it funny and dense and horribly difficult to get. All at the same time. It was fun, I didn't read all of it, but wherever I opened the page, I always found an allusion to something that made sense to me.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_114170873" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114170873" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114170873?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8260591-finnegans-wake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=pogue-mahone">pogue-mahone</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114170873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1988022347720957118">Let me explain the five-star rating. When I was teenager I was ludicrously shy. I was the son and heir of a shyness that was criminally vulgar. My all-conquering shyness kept Morrissey in gold-plated ormolu swans for eight years. Any contact with human beings made me mumble in horror and scuttle off to lurk in dark corners. But I developed this automatic writing technique in school to ease my mounting stress whenever teachers were poaching victims to answer questions, perform presentations or ge</span>
  <span id="freeText1988022347720957118" style="display:none">Let me explain the five-star rating. When I was teenager I was ludicrously shy. I was the son and heir of a shyness that was criminally vulgar. My all-conquering shyness kept Morrissey in gold-plated ormolu swans for eight years. Any contact with human beings made me mumble in horror and scuttle off to lurk in dark corners. But I developed this automatic writing technique in school to ease my mounting stress whenever teachers were poaching victims to answer questions, perform presentations or generally humiliate. I would start out composing a piece of surrealist free-association prose, usually violently satirical. As the teachers (or pupils or other humans) closed in around me, my prose would lapse into soothing gibberish. Sometimes I wrote a stream of pretty sounding words (I was a rabid sesquipedalian in my teens)—zeugmatic, antediluvian, milquetoast, mugwump. Luscious lovely words! Sometimes language broke down into neologisms or gibberish—boobleplop, artycary, frumpalerp, etc. Nervy, throbbing syllables. I came to associate collapsed language with an inner space where I went to hide from the imagined humiliations of interacting with others. Once I escaped the imprisonment of my inner conscious (over a four-year period known as The Torture Years), I always used nonsense writing as a means of getting through difficult situations—where others might doodle, for example, I would write Joycean Jabberwocky. Still do, usually on the phone. So this book, to me, is <i>The Little Book of Calm</i>. Except it isn’t little, and it makes people shit themselves. Me? I love this magnificent beast. Unless you suffer from similar deep-seated psychological wounds that threaten to gradually consume your entire adult life, don’t read this. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1988022347720957118" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_87441836" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87441836" />
    <a title="Geoff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/126266-geoff"><img alt="Geoff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399505224p2/126266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87441836?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoff" href="/user/show/126266-geoff">Geoff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199695157" href="/book/show/14828979-finnegans-wake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126266-geoff?shelf=in-the-wake-of-the-wake">in-the-wake-of-the-wake</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126266-geoff?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126266-geoff?shelf=infinite-books">infinite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87441836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17691914519815439741"><i>Finnegans Wake</i> is Joyce’s masterpiece, the culmination of his life’s work, the apex of his art, the tremendous final achievement of the 20th century’s greatest prose stylist. To ignore Joyce’s masterpiece is to miss out on one of a handful of great events in literary history. <i>Dubliners</i> anticipated <i>A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</i>, <i>A Portrait of the Artist…</i> anticipated <i>Ulysses</i>, <i>Ulysses</i> anticipated <i>Finnegans Wake</i>. Joyce’s individual works are particularly momentous set side by side, as the </span>
  <span id="freeText17691914519815439741" style="display:none"><i>Finnegans Wake</i> is Joyce’s masterpiece, the culmination of his life’s work, the apex of his art, the tremendous final achievement of the 20th century’s greatest prose stylist. To ignore Joyce’s masterpiece is to miss out on one of a handful of great events in literary history. <i>Dubliners</i> anticipated <i>A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</i>, <i>A Portrait of the Artist…</i> anticipated <i>Ulysses</i>, <i>Ulysses</i> anticipated <i>Finnegans Wake</i>. Joyce’s individual works are particularly momentous set side by side, as the trajectory of his craft's transfiguration can be clearly traced. For Joyce, all roads led to the <i>Wake</i>. We cannot consider the snow “faintly falling, like the descent of their last end, upon all the living and the dead”, we cannot consider “Once upon a time and a very good time it was there was a moocow coming down along the road and this moocow that was coming down along the road met a nicens little boy named baby tuckoo”, we cannot consider “Stately, plump Buck Mulligan came from the stairhead, bearing a bowl of lather on which a mirror and a razor lay crossed”, without considering “A way a lone a last a loved a long the riverrun, past Eve and Adam's, from swerve of shore to bend of bay, brings us by a commodius vicus of recirculation back to Howth Castle and Environs.” To accept Joyce’s place in the history of literature is to accept <i>Finnegans Wake</i> as his greatest contribution. To ignore or dismiss it is to leave a gaping hole in your understanding of the progress of literary aesthetics in the modern age. As William Gass said: "<i>FW</i> is the high-water mark of Modernism, and not to have been fundamentally influenced by it as a writer is not to have lived in your time. Not to live in your time is a serious moral flaw."<br /><br /><i>Finnegans Wake</i>, at first blush, might seem the most uninviting literary relic imaginable. It begins in the middle of a fragment of a sentence and immediately immerses the reader in a floodtide of its <i>Wake</i>language without any ado- no lamp, lantern, or quickflickering guidepost torches to ease one’s way in and through. And, to be sure, this is a very difficult book, perhaps the most difficult book you or I will ever read. But let me here briefly comment on what the <i>Wake</i> is not: it is not gibberish, it is not “the product of a diseased mind”, it is not “an elaborate prank” to make fools of readers and academia. To be a proponent of any of these claims is to have not spent time with the text. It is to not trust that Joyce, after having perfected and exhausted the potentialities of the form of the novel with <i>Ulysses</i>, was capable of going beyond that achievement, to forge for himself and for us an utterly new way- to push the idea of the novel, and the language of the novel, past itself and into a new mode or form. What more would one person have to accomplish than write <i>Ulysses</i> to earn an audience’s confidence? <i>Finnegans Wake</i> is not only not unreadababble, it is perhaps the most carefully, minutely, complexly composed work of art the modern era has produced. A third of Joyce’s life was spent rendering the <i>Wake</i> into the form in which we have it now. Might we, as readers, not allow ourselves to be a fraction of a percent as generous with our time, to try to understand what he was attempting, on his terms?<br /><br />Enough of what the <i>Wake</i> isn’t, on to what it is. First of all, it is music. Second, it is an experimental prose work, a work whose form and content are one and the same, where there is no boundary between style and substance. Thirdly and onward, it is an occurrence of language. It is a vast palimpsest, a layering and weaving of etyms. It is the realization and perfection of the work of <i>static</i> art that Joyce was approaching his entire career- art, literature, that does not progress from point to point as in traditional narrative, but exists and is experienced in cycles, circles, reverberations, (re)generations, iterations, emergences, divested of the encumbrances of space and time. It is Flaubert’s ideal “book about nothing.”* It is the density and obscurity of night rendered into waterfall rainbow river language. “A permanent member of the avant-garde.” “An unpopularizable book.” A great riddle or maze. An amalgamation of gods. Obscure pun-drenched birdtongue, strangest little song you’ll ever damn hear. Hen scratchings on the magazine wall, typographically rendered, a polyvocal defence of the great shame and guilt of man. The tonguetwister of allhumanity dreamingwaking together, it was uncovered in a burial mound. Vico’s four horsemen of the arkpokalypse. Mamalujo broadcasting from the hill of Shaun and a donkey brayayaying over radio waves intercepted telling strange advertisements out of Carthage and burning Roam. It is the Egyptian Book of the Dead within the Book of Kells within the Old Testament and the New within Dante and Shakespeare and Milton and Goethe and Swift et Sterne et al. and Wilde’s trial to boot. It is a motley chorus composed of all of Ireland’s saints and sinners. “Then’s now with now’s then in tense continuant. Heard.” It is Finn MacCool’s salmon flitting in the deep well and Tristan and Isolde’s marriage ship sailing out under the cry of gulls (forewaves whisping whshhpwshpshp) and at the same time it is their opera made prose, mild und leise. It is the excrement ink writ on the foolscap flesh of Shem the Penman, thus it is Shemdean spawn. It is Anna Livia Plurabelle’s missive to the antagonistic greater world of chitterchattererflitterflatterers (how loathe they have become to me!) and little Issy star cloud sister’s spilled milk across the great nightspan, bababbling brooks about the laying mountainous mass of sleepman. Treacling trickling trickster tome, laplapping gossip and news, soundbites and screams and dieatribes from Lucifer’s caindom, enabler of murtherer, and also song of the cockcrowcoolicolala! Noman’s humming in the valley of the wal. Shaft of light pierce o'reillying the mourning mist. A confuscation of mystification by utteration and ululation with confabulation and iteration of vocalization of a Wake in Preegress! Hush! Caution! Echoland! What a funferall! The last lief on the stonetree. “The untireties of livesliving being the one substrance of a streamsbecoming. Totalled in toldteld and teldtold in tittletell tattle.” Mind your hats goan in!<br /><br />Lastly, <i>Finnegans Wake</i> is the least pessimistic book I know. After one has accustomed oneself to the night language, after one is acquainted with Joyce’s modes and methods, this book is pure joy. One begins to anticipate the moments and emergence of themes, iterations of characters in different guises, developments and repetitions of rhythms, word and sound groupings that recur in exact placement, much as one listens to a beloved symphony or opera. The music of the <i>Wake</i>, like a true Irish wake, is a rejoicing at the deathbed, rounds of songs rollicking the departed soul into the next cycle of existence. What is more optimistic than Joyce’s interpretation of Vico’s historical cycles? That as we approach our non-being the clock resets, time ticks ahead again for us among the shades of history, the sun rises as it always will, the night dissipates, the fog of this dream-life clears and mankind emerges again, to suffer it all, sing it all, weep through it all, live it all again. That these ages resound again and again not through great men only but through everyman, that the resurrection of the meaning of man comes in the simplest of assemblages- husband, wife, son, daughter. This affirmation is a mainstay throughout all of Joyce’s work- that the universal erupts through the banal, that the commonplace is the point where the cosmos enacts its drama. What could be a more joyous celebration and confirmation, not only of human life as it emerges from the darkness of meaninglessness in the only possible way it can, through language, but of the creative life in particular, the life whose purpose is to make new forms out of the fragments of the old, to anticipate the new, to instill a beautiful renewal of purpose for each emerging epoch, that it might know its own language, make its own music?<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281479658?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Nathan's review</a> is a fount of information, please do visit the museyroom. Tip!<br /><br />And if you are abcedminded, when you set out on your own reeding of the <i>Wake</i>, please to be joining and contributing to the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/124430-finnegans-wake-grappa" rel="nofollow noopener">Wake Grappa</a>. We're all of us over there at different points on the turning of the widening gyre, so feel free to hop on at any time.<br /><br />*The entire plot of <i>Finnegans Wake</i> can be summed up essentially in that classic cliched opening phrase “It was a dark and stormy night…”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17691914519815439741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_898882899" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/898882899?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199695157" href="/book/show/14828979-finnegans-wake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=treasured-reading-experience">treasured-reading-experience</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=review-may-contain-comic-content">review-may-contain-comic-content</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=joyce-and-related">joyce-and-related</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer898882899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15759011050167787307"><b>The Slalom of Joyledge<br/><br/>Howto scaledown this Beschova finntail<br/>This filletov beginnings that sings of all endings,<br/>This pest of a pal in jest<br/>And bad cess to you, Joyking<br/>For the reeding is tufftuff<br/>But the prize is the laffing<br/>Tho low in the belly<br/>It sores with the learning<br/>Of finnglish and jinglish <br/>Pigeon linguish and djoytisch<br/><br/>Ten stories tall<br/>And twenty the deepings<br/>some to the writeoff<br/>And Moore to the leftings<br/>Finn’s houseful of hawsers <br/>And hods and their spilling<br/>Give Humpty his tallwall<br/>And role in all f</b></span>
  <span id="freeText15759011050167787307" style="display:none"><b>The Slalom of Joyledge<br /><br />Howto scaledown this Beschova finntail<br />This filletov beginnings that sings of all endings,<br />This pest of a pal in jest<br />And bad cess to you, Joyking<br />For the reeding is tufftuff<br />But the prize is the laffing<br />Tho low in the belly<br />It sores with the learning<br />Of finnglish and jinglish <br />Pigeon linguish and djoytisch<br /><br />Ten stories tall<br />And twenty the deepings<br />some to the writeoff<br />And Moore to the leftings<br />Finn’s houseful of hawsers <br />And hods and their spilling<br />Give Humpty his tallwall<br />And role in all fallings<br />Atomnal, Printernal<br />Summerian, Hibernial<br /><br />Story forth into bygones<br />O Joyking of spieling<br />Ewe raddle us with riddles<br />Till we’re red in the blushers<br />Veins vulging in temples<br />And grey matter smarting<br />We reed in the rushes <br />Of joycfull mehinding<br />Seepon, seepunder,<br />pong of pondyman<br /><br />Thru hart strings and wordlings<br />And lingo lang twanging<br />Ewe bleat all the sorrel<br />Of wars evel waging<br />In valleys, on hillsides<br />In shore water rising <br />Tho miss chiefs and piss takes<br />Give rest from sorratelling<br />spoofon, spoofonder,<br />sham of shemyman.<br /><br />Futurepresent pastperfect<br />As the river at her rising<br />The trees bend to bog<br />From the turf seeds fresh reedlings<br />Men breed new wars<br />As old wars reseeding<br />Bodies for battles<br />Procreation creating<br />Weepon, weeponder,<br />Song of sorrowmon<br /><br />Atom, Eve and their childer<br />The first family feuding<br />Cain abling his sister<br />Edem for all triblings<br />In cest and in jest <br />The story ewer spouring<br />By yon labious banks <br />And by perchypole sarding<br />thru noughty times ever<br />And foriver insemenating<br /><br />O Batterfull of codlogicals <br />O Senchus Mór pranKing<br />Exagminating yore glosses <br />Yore musikers and blarneying<br />French rhymes, Moore's chimes<br />Jack's house ever building<br />Alicetella's fun essay<br />Swift Sternley past teaching<br />Reminding this scribbler<br />To finnish vociferating<br />Now's nunc or nimmer!</b><br />............................................ <br /><br />The following commentaries and glosses may help understanding:<br />(all words in italics occur in both <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11013.Finnegans_Wake" title="Finnegans Wake by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Finnegans Wake</a> and 'The Slalom of Joyledge';<br />all words underlined occur only in 'The Slalom of Joyledge')<br /><br /><u>The Slalom of Joyledge</u>: the Salmon of Knowledge was a mythical fish from the Fenian cycle of legends, and was thought to embody all the knowledge of the world. Joyce's book is full of knowledge, and reading it is a steep learning curve.<br /><br /><u>Beschoffs</u>: a fish shop established in Dublin in 1913, which was famous for <u>cod</u> fillets in batter. <br /><u>scaledown</u>: remove scales, reduce, descend a slope<br />'Bad <i>
  <u>cess</u>
</i> to you' is a curse, recalling the medieval cess pit.<br /><br />Tim Finnegan, in the ballad 'Finnegan’s Wake', was a hod carrier on a building site who fell off a wall and died. He was <b>resurrected</b> when whiskey accidentally fell on his lips during his wake.<br /><i>Humphrey</i>, one of the main characters in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11013.Finnegans_Wake" title="Finnegans Wake by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Finnegans Wake</a>, himself suffers a fall and a <b>resurrection</b>.<br /><i>
  <u>Humpty</u>
</i> Dumpty, also mentioned in FW, famously fell off a wall.<br />The series of falls recall The Fall of Man.<br /><br /><i>
  <u>Sard</u>
</i> is a reference to fish, and is alternative slang for a four letter word beginning with f.<br /><i>
  <u>Perchypole</u>
</i> : a fishing rod or other type of rod/pole.<br /><br /><i>
  <u>Raddle</u>
</i>: red colouring on <i>
  <u>ewes</u>
</i> to mark their encounter with the ram.<br /><i>
  <u>Sorrel</u>
</i> or red clover: a plant that causes infertility in sheep.<br /><br /><u>Joyking</u> : James Joyce or 'King James Joyce', author of the Finnegans Wake bible of sorts. <br /><i>Pranquean</i>: Joyce’s name for the 16th Irish pirate queen Granuaile, also known as Gráinne Ní Mháile or Grace O'Malley, who was a blithe borrower from traders along the west coast of Ireland and known for her sense of humour. <br /><u>PranKing</u>: my alternative name for James Joyce, himself a pirateer and plagiarist since he was a blithe borrower of words and ideas, and a purveyor of every manner of jest and wordplay. <br /><br />The <i>
  <u>Senchus Mór</u>
</i>, referred to in Finnegans Wake, is a 5th century account of the Brehon laws of Ireland, written in ancient dialect. It contains many later commentaries and glosses inserted between the lines and in the margins. It is the perfect metaphor for Finnegans Wake, itself a corpus of Irish history, written in what sometimes seems like obscure dialect, and which contains commentaries inserted throughout the text and glosses in the margins. <br /><br /><i>Shem the penman</i>: Joyce’s pseudonym for himself in the Wake. Shem is the brother of Shaun and Izzy, and son of Humphrey and Anna, the first family around whom the action revolves.<br /><br /><i>Shaun</i>: represents Irish Nationalism. Shaun is partly inspired by the character 'Shaun the Post' in Dion Boucicault’s 19th century play <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12593762.Arrah_Na_Pogue" title="Arrah Na Pogue by Dion Boucicault" rel="nofollow noopener">Arrah Na Pogue</a>. <br /><br /><i>Izzy</i>, sister of Shem and Shaun represents a series of female figures in history, mythology and literature. She is: <br />	<i>Isolde</i>: legendary figure betrothed to King Mark who eloped with the younger Tristan; <br />	Grainne: legendary figure betrothed to Fionn Mac Cumhal who eloped with young Diarmuid; <br />	Deirdre of the Sorrows: princess of Ulster whose beauty caused war and destruction;<br />	<i>Fionnuala</i>: daughter of the sea god Mannanán Mac Lir who was banished into exile;<br />	<i>
  <u>Alice</u>
</i> Liddell: Lewis Carrol's young friend; <br />	<i>
  <u>Stella</u>
</i>: Esther Johnson, Jonathan Swift’s young friend; <br />	<i>
  <u>Vanessa</u>
</i>, Esther Vanhomrigh, also associated with Swift.<br /><br /><i>Anna</i>, wife of Humphrey and mother of Izzy, Shem and Shaun. She is also known as Anna Livia Plurabelle. She is the personification of the river Liffey which flows out to the sea at Dublin Bay. In the last lines of the book she transforms into the sea god Mannanán's daughter, Fionnuala, exiled to the Sea of Moyle for centuries.<br /><br /><i>Humphrey</i>, her husband, is the personification of the Hill of Howth, a horn of land on the north edge of Dublin city which thrusts into Dublin bay. Humphrey also represents Fionn Mac Cumhal and other mythological and historical figures.<br /><br />Percy <u>French</u> and Thomas <u>Moore</u>: two nineteenth century song writers whose 'songs of yore' recur in Joyce's text. Moore's ‘Silent, O Moyle’, for example is used to tell of the sea god's daughter’s exile to the Sea of Moyle. Other popular songs, rhymes and doggerel feature frequently in the text, in particular, the rhyme known as 'The House that Jack Built'.<br /><br />Everything in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11013.Finnegans_Wake" title="Finnegans Wake by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Finnegans Wake</a> has several meanings and while the meanings are often camouflaged, they are nevertheless reinforced through constant layering. Many things which have been said in other texts are unsaid in 'Finnegans Wake', as in taken apart, remade, further dismantled, further refurbished in a continuos cycle similar to the geological ages of the world. Joyce’s text is therefore a <u>palimpsest</u> in every sense of the word, a veritable <i>Geoglyphy</i> carved out of history.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15759011050167787307" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1774589830306777737" style="display:none">I take no shame in admitting that I cannot read this book. <span id="freeTextContainer7764586656134494739"><i>"Wipe your glosses with what you know."</i><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451880765i/17616134._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='306' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>I tend never to retread the same book twice. I finish a novel or a book, digest it, then move on. Having just finished 'Finnegans Wake' I'm not sure that approach is even possible. This is a book that is simply impossible to really finish. Yes, I read from the beginning to end. Yes, I listened to it while reading. Yes, I spoke sentences out loud. Yes, I shouted words. Yes, I underlined phrases that tickled and rhymes that ringed. But, I feel like I've scra</span>
  <span id="freeText7764586656134494739" style="display:none"><i>"Wipe your glosses with what you know."</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451880765i/17616134._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="306" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>I tend never to retread the same book twice. I finish a novel or a book, digest it, then move on. Having just finished 'Finnegans Wake' I'm not sure that approach is even possible. This is a book that is simply impossible to really finish. Yes, I read from the beginning to end. Yes, I listened to it while reading. Yes, I spoke sentences out loud. Yes, I shouted words. Yes, I underlined phrases that tickled and rhymes that ringed. But, I feel like I've scratched the semantic surface of a great field. I'm not sure when I'll return, but I'm pretty certain that the gravity is there. I feel it even as I gladly set this book aside. This is a novel that demands attention. It frustrates and confuses the most diligent seeker. I never felt in control. I never felt in command. I was in the river, and floated for a time and am just happy I didn't drown. It is world I will return to like a dream-filled sleep when the day is done and night returns. </span>

  <span id="freeText9162640187744182873" style="display:none">The other day we saw <i>The Ghost</i>, the rather fine new movie by Polanski. Ewan McGregor plays a ghostwriter, who's been brought in to fix up the memoirs of a British ex-Prime Minister who absolutely isn't Tony Blair. He's given the manuscript, and groans in pain.<br /><br />"That bad?" asks the woman who isn't Cherie Blair.<br /><br />"Well it's got all the words," says McGregor. "They're just not in the right order."<br /><br />This suggested to me the following simple experiment with <i>Finnegans Wake</i>, one of the greatest etc etc in the English language. I downloaded an electronic version from the Web and wrote a little script. It calculates statistics for the frequencies of each letter conditioned on the three preceding ones, then produces random text using that model. And here's a sample of what comes out:<blockquote>rivin's Fleperumpholature, puirel from the going beamstroki, genes aultealsion! Captermidcauting. Allfishe'll theiresendt all, andfall the blisation (for of ally witnes of coulminus. Blugger, wher nutbrings my los oned? Mch? What sting up funnies! Huddamsome Bankata, the keter soother sets the beloomostes, sable us in duallects neupon, wholy not does! Exceed in siltop as taned mucheepiworder aflute. While store your bred welchups we kitch oxbell, old som! Curly vale. The scenty view (the our civisierengracles was dupshua milisquewing bransisterrand the knobo, prise fall knacordy) and picky karu? Yip! I sait is, worts fore fassoo thath they speechappy inted that bit thall kning to thehry. For the like fing of the untill Buggedy Acreside? Bygmour flatehaun sore! But a cal, them doland up (and you, perfor virging of the Gachind lilt and supping's the that the saint, him my brade rainpleave you abothe king Jerospears forews wer's vitrodalths vitation abou remen thorly wated bease, there lit is like the Lucat wattern-his in thing hone: he willwho it bynemberumphs, faraden, here they sail nought of the sweet-puls temple of are whirk and eld not and Palm aro! This evers, Exmoonanture, thead fied and too tron the lanagain ther! Marre! Kevitutterod. Shaughter of Eons, Potter rud of thin collow. One to beehights headlos he gue. Dalilitopspes hers and a Noho. All to evers scan night!<br /><br />Juva: Sod the thurch he breated! And the ming's my schlucises lausan the coy Brael mudder Sever, a his nakewdy feat Bashoweriful and it feet to mire blowsome, thems bis!</blockquote>OK, I admit it's not as accomplished as the original. But if you brought in a competent ghostwriter and gave him a month to improve it, who knows? The random generator has created some promising lines. I quite like "For the like fing of the untill Buggedy Acreside?", and "Juva: Sod the thurch he breated!" seems interestingly blasphemous...</span>

<div id="review_9360692" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9360692" />
    <a title="Kelly McCubbin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/287449-kelly-mccubbin"><img alt="Kelly McCubbin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1195589246p2/287449.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9360692?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly McCubbin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly McCubbin" href="/user/show/287449-kelly-mccubbin">Kelly McCubbin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9360692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16234962622496302681">The easiest book in the world... seriously. With scholars unable to ever reach consensus on what the book is or how it should be read or even if it actually has value, you can simply ignore them. Your opinions are just as valid. Add to this the wads of cultural ephemera that Joyce has packed the book with and you find yourself in the rare position to occasionally be BETTER qualified to interpret parts of the text than academics.<br/>Try this, get some friends together, pop the cork on a few bottles o</span>
  <span id="freeText16234962622496302681" style="display:none">The easiest book in the world... seriously. With scholars unable to ever reach consensus on what the book is or how it should be read or even if it actually has value, you can simply ignore them. Your opinions are just as valid. Add to this the wads of cultural ephemera that Joyce has packed the book with and you find yourself in the rare position to occasionally be BETTER qualified to interpret parts of the text than academics.<br />Try this, get some friends together, pop the cork on a few bottles of wine and, in your most twee Irish accents read it to each other. A whole new world of dirty jokes, awful puns, barbed insults and musical references will suddenly pop out of this previously "impenetrable" text.<br />And don't be afraid to get sidetracked, it's part of the point.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16234962622496302681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_50782093" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50782093" />
    <a title="Nicholas Karpuk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1574263-nicholas-karpuk"><img alt="Nicholas Karpuk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442509877p2/1574263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50782093?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicholas Karpuk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicholas Karpuk" href="/user/show/1574263-nicholas-karpuk">Nicholas Karpuk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141181264" href="/book/show/442140.Finnegans_Wake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1574263-nicholas-karpuk?shelf=surrendered">surrendered</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50782093" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11412138538005922299">This is not a fair score, I'll admit it right up front. This book affirms my reasoning for reading the first few pages of a book before buying it. This I bought because I've been trying to read more classics, but my experience has shown me that classics shouldn't be exempted from the first few page practice.<br/><br/>Here's the second paragraph of the book:<br/><br/>"Sir Tristram, violer d'amores, fr'over the short sea, had passen-core rearrived from North Armorica on this side the scraggy isthmus of Europe Minor t</span>
  <span id="freeText11412138538005922299" style="display:none">This is not a fair score, I'll admit it right up front. This book affirms my reasoning for reading the first few pages of a book before buying it. This I bought because I've been trying to read more classics, but my experience has shown me that classics shouldn't be exempted from the first few page practice.<br /><br />Here's the second paragraph of the book:<br /><br />"Sir Tristram, violer d'amores, fr'over the short sea, had passen-core rearrived from North Armorica on this side the scraggy isthmus of Europe Minor to wilderfight his penisolate war: nor had topsawyers rocks by the stream Oconee exaggerated themselse to Laurens county's gogios while they went doublin their mumper all the time: nor avoice from afire bellowsed mishe mishe to taugtaug thuartpeatrick: not yet, though venissoon after, had a kidscad buttended a bland old isaac: not yet, though all's fair in vanessy, were sosie sesthers wroth with twone nathondjoe. Rot a peck of pa's malt had Jhem or Shen brewed by arclight and rory end to the regginbrow was to be seen ringsome on the aquaface."<br /><br />I've read a few books with a confusing intro. It's a common practice in sci-fi, where you throw the reader in head first with mysterious nouns and let them work it out as they go. This is not the case, because after a few pages I realized Mr. Joyce wasn't going to start making anymore sense than he already was. Here's a quote from page 311, when I skipped ahead to see if it was all like this:<br /><br />"It was long after once there was a lealand in the luffing ore it was less after lives thor a toyler in the tawn at all ohr it was note before he drew out the moddle of Kersse by jerkin his dressing but and or it was not before athwartships he buttonhaled the Norweeger's capstan."<br /><br />I considered this might be a phonetic thing, similar to what Twain used, but no matter how I tried with the first few pages I could not parse it into anything comprehensible. Even Canterbury Tales has the decency to make sense when read aloud. If I can not comprehend a book on a sentence level, a paragraph level, or a chapter level I'm just going to give up.<br /><br />Here's the first sentence of the intro, which I went back to look at after throwing the book in the trash, feeling bad, and retrieving it:<br /><br />"There is no agreement as to what Finnegans Wake is about, whether or not it is "about" anything, or even whether it is, in any sense of the word, "readable".<br /><br />Oh good. That's very encouraging. Here's the thing, I don't read as a challenge. I read for ideas, or to be entertained. I like to read books with ideas I might struggle with, it's fun to think about it. I do not like fighting to choke down the words themselves. Joyce makes up words, uses dialect, and god knows what else in the first few chapters, it's like he's trying to be obtuse to make a game out of it.<br /><br />That might be fun in a short story, but this is 600-some pages.<br /><br />If someone can suggest a good method for consuming this damn thing, the Rosetta Stone for why I should care, I'm open to ideas, but otherwise, it's getting put away</span>
  <a data-text-id="11412138538005922299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_434410156" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/434410156" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/434410156?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2012)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=irish">irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=sex">sex</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=doorstopper">doorstopper</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=funny">funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=family-drama">family-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=challenging">challenging</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer434410156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9856798405862009314">Looks daunting, unintelligible and incomprehensible at first. However, read it aloud and with open mind and the meaning <i>might</i> come down on you. I said "might" because no matter how much thinking I put on some of the paragraphs or lines, some meanings seemed so obscure and I had no choice but to let them stay that way.<br/><br/>Still I found this book amazing. It is one of its kind. What amazed me really was its play of words. Unmatched. Never seen before. Close to it so far is Anthony Burgess's <i>Clockwork</i> </span>
  <span id="freeText9856798405862009314" style="display:none">Looks daunting, unintelligible and incomprehensible at first. However, read it aloud and with open mind and the meaning <i>might</i> come down on you. I said "might" because no matter how much thinking I put on some of the paragraphs or lines, some meanings seemed so obscure and I had no choice but to let them stay that way.<br /><br />Still I found this book amazing. It is one of its kind. What amazed me really was its play of words. Unmatched. Never seen before. Close to it so far is Anthony Burgess's <i>Clockwork</i> but it seems like kindergarten level to Joyce's masteral degree. Joyce used what they call as "portmanteau" or the fusing together of two or more words in the same or different languages. Thus "kissmiss" is both the festive season and something that might happen during it, with a suggestion of fatefulness; the Holy Father becomes a "hoary frother"; and an old photo is a "fadograph." Reading this book requires Job's patience but in the end, it is rewarding for the fact that this is another testimony to James Joyce's brilliance as a writer. <i>Finnegans Wake</i> is the playful luminous moon to <i>Ulysses'</i> serious bright sun. One complement the other like flaunting to the world that James Joyce could be funny after writing the very profound retelling of Homer's classic epic poem, <i>Odyssey.</i> <br /><br />I admit that at some point, I thought I would not be able to finish this. I thought of giving up after two chapters and I did not understanding ANYTHING. I felt like I was just wasting my time. However, a GR friend advised me to read write ups in the internet and it helped. I referred to the internet after reading each chapter or part of it. There were times when I could not correlate the two so I let that pass too. That approach of reading a chapter of the book then refer to the internet helped because at least I was picking up the basic plot. Still, it was confusing. The stories in it seemed not connected to each other and there was no main plot. It was only towards the end when I realized that James Joyce was not telling one story but many, as many as 17 according to Wiki. The most ubiquitous, among the 17, is a story of a fall that turns out not to be entirely negative, including the Fall of Man; an indiscretion in Phoenix Park, Dublin, a sex scandal involving an older man and two girls; and a tumble (<i>Humpty Dumpty,</i> yes that children's song) from a ladder by an Irish builder, <b>Tim Finnegan.</b> <br /><br />This book offers just a different kind of reading experience. One of its kind. It is amazing how James Joyce put together this book and wrote all those verses that are so funny. Definitely brilliant. <br /><br />And oh I love that unfinished sentence in the end that goes back to the first sentence. When I started reading and saw the first sentence that looked truncated, I was thrown off immediately and shrieked <i>"What is this?"</i> but I said if I was able to finish <i>Ulysses</i>, I should be able to read this one too. Of course, the big encouragement of my GR friends motivated me to continue reading just in time for my daughter to ask this library copy back.<br /><br />(I now see some of those GR friends liking this review, so I better stop now.) Thank you so much, GR friends!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9856798405862009314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17148367340985659590" style="display:none">Stealing an idea from Manny's review, here's part of the (British) Highway Code if it was written by James Joyce any time during the last 17 years of his life. You should have a kelchy chose and clayblade and at all times make prayses to the three of clubs always, having checked it is not misleading to tuss like a whoopy anisine, whipping your eyesoult and gnatsching your teats over the brividdy road users before changing course or direction, stopping or moving off (djowl there, longfoot here, and bejesus back again) and the doctor's bill for Joe McJohn and all his catholic lemony heathens.<br /><br />Cancel them after use, the rancid old patootsies. If, for instance, you want to stop adilly and look adolly, as we do, as we done and as we will, do not blooger the whooger untill you pass the galoshes of Mrs Minchum Birny Kirny and the little jeepy twins.   <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/554174065" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780141192291" href="/book/show/13933665-the-restored-finnegans-wake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=exert-yourself">exert-yourself</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=joycean-tales-of-brave-ulysses">joycean-tales-of-brave-ulysses</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer554174065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10959820366289868316"><b>Prelured to a Nocturnal Pleasure</b><br/><br/><i>"It isn't a matter of submitting uncritically to a difficult work; it's about trusting that the artist knows what he/she is doing, even if you don't apprehend it right away. Just keep reading: even the most difficult novel will eventually make some sense, and if you realise you've missed things, you can always go back for a second try if still curious...some people like a challenge...some people are open to new, initially puzzling experiences...": Steven Moore</i><br/><br/><br/><b>Thir</b></span>
  <span id="freeText10959820366289868316" style="display:none"><b>Prelured to a Nocturnal Pleasure</b><br><br><i>"It isn't a matter of submitting uncritically to a difficult work; it's about trusting that the artist knows what he/she is doing, even if you don't apprehend it right away. spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]> <div id="review_1405561739" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1405561739" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1405561739?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781840226614" href="/book/show/13503979-finnegans-wake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1405561739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9540927980384716578">Everybody knows the plot of <i>Finnegans Wake</i>. Rich, old man Finnegan has died, leaving behind no will and no direct heirs. A riotous comedy of errors ensues at his wake (an open-casket affair), where his extended family and business associates (a collection of colourful, conniving characters to say the least), vie for supremacy, each one plotting and scheming to inherit Finnegan’s vast business empire and considerable real estate portfolio, which features amongst numerous holdings the grand and op</span>
  <span id="freeText9540927980384716578" style="display:none">Everybody knows the plot of <i>Finnegans Wake</i>. Rich, old man Finnegan has died, leaving behind no will and no direct heirs. A riotous comedy of errors ensues at his wake (an open-casket affair), where his extended family and business associates (a collection of colourful, conniving characters to say the least), vie for supremacy, each one plotting and scheming to inherit Finnegan’s vast business empire and considerable real estate portfolio, which features amongst numerous holdings the grand and opulent Howth castle and environs, whose grounds so wonderfully brought to life by Joyce’s vivid descriptions, form the setting of the novel. But it’s the characters in <i>Finnegans Wake</i> that really stand out. Who can forget cruel Mr Snood, whose Machiavellian machinations can be seen beneath each interaction, or doddering old Mrs Buttercup, whose innocent bumbling antics serve to hilariously thwart his plans at every step. The uproarious hijinks come to a head with the famous twist in the final act, whereupon Finnegan arises from his casket before stunned guests, proclaiming the words, <i>“Finnegan wakes!”</i>, revealing the whole thing to have been nothing more than an elaborate ruse. Finnegan, having lain conscious in state the entire time, and having witnessed the appalling behaviour of his associates and potential heirs, decides to leave the entire estate to a previously unmentioned boy named Billy, the simpleminded child of a village milk-maid, who having some months ago touched Finnegan’s heart with a simple unassuming gesture of kindness (this act, which is recounted by Finnegan at this point in the narrative – Billy’s offering to a parched and lonely Finnegan of a glass of milk and a kind shoulder - has been variously analysed and interpreted by critics since the novel’s publication, being a particularly poignant moment, rich in symbolism and moral significance), has revealed to Finnegan - an otherwise stingy and curmudgeonly man - the importance of kindness and friendship, a lesson upon which Finnegan now intends to reform his own life, beginning this very moment, by expelling each and every parasitic so-called friend directly from the castle, <i>“beyond a swerve of shore and bend of bay, past Eve and Adam’s, and back along riverrun whence they came!”</i> The novel’s cathartic climax and its heart-warming closing scene in which Billy and his mother Daisy, upon arriving at the castle, realise their life of hard poverty is finally at an end – their tearful embrace, and Daisy’s brief monologue which concludes the novel, spoken in endearing and authentic local dialect (<i>“Ooer Billy, I reckon I’ve tugged me last teat” </i>) – are some of the most powerful and enduring moments in all of literature.<br /><br /><i>Finnegans Wake</i> was published in 1939 to a confused and divided critical reception, its uncomplicated and light-hearted comedic tone representing a surprising departure from the author’s earlier, far more serious works. Rumour has it that Joyce had for some time been working on an alternative version of the novel, one almost entirely concerned with puns and wordplay, filled with obscure and esoteric references, and devoid of any persistent characters or coherent plot. But fortunately Joyce was moved by close friends to abandon that enterprise, it all being quite silly and pretentious really, and who would want to read a book like that anyway.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9540927980384716578" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_281479658" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281479658" />
    <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis"><img alt="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548295774p2/7607437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281479658?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis">Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780141192291" href="/book/show/13933665-the-restored-finnegans-wake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=rejoyce-again-again">rejoyce-again-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=annotations-please">annotations-please</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=100-mccaffery-read">100-mccaffery-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=unreadababble">unreadababble</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=apostrophe">apostrophe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=2014-gelesen">2014-gelesen</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281479658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14008063754570682238">Was bin you? :: <br/>Ein luger ; faelscher ; Father of ; flibber flabber ; Miss MacLeader ; desimulate ; hazug ; trick a her stir ; leogere ; false wit ; phonitical ; cheet a puma ; con ; equal vadar ; story hearer ; promotorcross ; mensoganto ; rascal ; hṛṣi ; hyper cryter ; Hair Pseudo ; mwongo ; path and logical ; dish o nest and storter ; libel and label ; not a squarestraight shooter ; counterfèting ; defamé ; calumniacator ; ;<br/><br/>Porce? Vava Varoom? Howso? :: <br/>I say I confirm I assert I am truthtosa</span>
  <span id="freeText14008063754570682238" style="display:none">Was bin you? :: <br />Ein luger ; faelscher ; Father of ; flibber flabber ; Miss MacLeader ; desimulate ; hazug ; trick a her stir ; leogere ; false wit ; phonitical ; cheet a puma ; con ; equal vadar ; story hearer ; promotorcross ; mensoganto ; rascal ; hṛṣi ; hyper cryter ; Hair Pseudo ; mwongo ; path and logical ; dish o nest and storter ; libel and label ; not a squarestraight shooter ; counterfèting ; defamé ; calumniacator ; ;<br /><br />Porce? Vava Varoom? Howso? :: <br />I say I confirm I assert I am truthtosay Allalivia <i>Finnegans Wake</i> durchaus and straight through and Whole Thing to have geread I claim. Hand on heart!<br /><br />Juan? :: <br />Juan the Second, anni dom twenty und twelve bisunto gesters this our lawd’s year of twenty eat foreteens, a certain day of a month of showers the very tens and ones of onehundredelv. Zwar. None and sept weeks of wakes in the woche. <br /><br />ού; :: <br />Outsea dorsea mostlich. In char. Avec avis and kittehs.<br /><br />V :: <br />Meet McPugh and McQue. Con Campbell. Wort by wort, healthy yeasties.<br /><br />Quant? :: <br />Fourmal mahlzeit meistlich. <br /><br />Qual odor? :: Yes well donkey shay! I furt her make of a claim to have been being udder stooding of a it. Maistro! And am therefore and thuswhy dubbel accused as.<br /><br />Who? :: <br />Finn MacCool!!!!!<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Otter stuff from primeval daze. Donnerwetter!<br />_______________<br />The final four pages of the Anna Livia chapter (I, 8) were rendered into Basic English by C.K. Ogden with the cooperation of Joyce. McHugh’s Annotations incorporate some of the notes made by Joyce for Ogden. See Ogden’s book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1021235.The_General_Basic_English_Dictionary" title="The General Basic English Dictionary by C.K. Ogden" rel="nofollow noopener">The General Basic English Dictionary</a>. I have not been able to track down Ogden’s rendering. See also the article “Universalizing Languages: <i>Finnegans Wake</i> Meets Basic English,” by Susan Shaw Sailer: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jstor.org/discover/10.2307/25474090?uid=3739856&amp;uid=2129&amp;uid=2&amp;uid=70&amp;uid=4&amp;uid=3739256&amp;sid=21101667991817">http://www.jstor.org/discover/10.2307...</a><br /><br /><br /><br />______________________<br />One might suspect that some remarks made regarding The Wake are in reality directed toward the 15th century Voynich Manuscript. Here’s the slowdown-lowdown:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nationalpost.com/m/wp/full-comment/blog.html?b=fullcomment.nationalpost.com/2012/11/16/scott-van-wynsberghe-deciphering-the-mysterious-voynich-manuscript">http://www.nationalpost.com/m/wp/full...</a><br /><br />“Despite their skills, not one of the above was able to read the manuscript, leading to the growing suspicion that the text does not involve any encryption, because that would have been broken by now. And it may not even involve any actual language, either: In 2004, the aforementioned Gordon Rugg declared that the text was just gibberish — an ancient hoax possibly assembled through a non-functional version of a Renaissance coding technique called the “Cardan grille.” Rugg could not, however, explain why anyone in the Renaissance would do such a thing, nor did he address the amazing effort that went into the hundreds of illustrations. After a century of study, the Voynich Manuscript still mocks us.”<br /><br />and the manuscript itself has been webbified:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://beinecke.library.yale.edu/digitallibrary/voynich.html">http://beinecke.library.yale.edu/digi...</a><br /><br /><br /><br />_______________<br />How does Finneganian work? Allow <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=e29k-F5jBpk" rel="nofollow noopener">Data to explain</a>. Joyce tends to keep his words to "ten or fewer" at a time. Curious that one of Data's pieces is from Mozart, albeit a symphony, because one is reminded of the scene from <i>Amadeus</i> in which Mozart explicates the possibilities contained within the operatic genre of writing multiple voices singing <i>simultaneously</i>, something which, in ordinary circumstances, is impossible when one word must <i>follow</i> its prior word. In Finneganian we can have them all at once. And not only do we have "ten or fewer words" at a time, we also have ten or more <i>characters</i> and stories at a time. The figures of our human history are all here, all at once: Here Comes Everybody!!!!<br /><br />Does anyone still believe that the English language is a monolingual language? Is English not already a Finneganian?<br /><br /><br /><br />____________<br />A video in which a few words of Joseph Campbell regarding The Wake are read over pretty images. [thanks to Friend Nick for bringing this to my attention).<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?feature=player_embedded&amp;v=cRvRDpH9OIY">https://www.youtube.com/watch?feature...</a><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />____________<br />Thoughts on Joyce from Slavoj Žižek’s [book:The Indivisible Remainder: On Schelling and Related Matters|90563], Verso, 2007. p 202.<br /><br />“This is also what Lacan’s thesis on ‘Joyce-the-symptom’ aims at: Joyce’s famous statement that he wrote <i>Finnegans Wake</i> in order to keep literary historians busy for the next four hundred years has to be read against the background of Lacan’s assertion that within a psychoanalytic cure a symptom is always addressed to the analyst and, as such, points forward towards its interpretation. The ‘modernism’ of Joyce resides in the fact that his works--at least <i>Ulysses</i> and <i>Finnegans Wake</i>--are not simply external to their interpretation but, as it were, take into account in advance their possible interpretations, and enter into dialogue with them. In so far as an interpretation or a theoretical explanation of a work of art endeavours to ‘frame’ its object, one can say that this modernist dialectics provides another example of how the frame is always included in, is a part of, the framed content: in modernism, a theory about the work is comprised in the work, the work is a kind of pre-emptive strike at possible theories about itself.<br /><br />“On that account, it is inappropriate to criticize Joyce for no longer writing for a naive reader capable of an immediate consumption of his works but for a reflected reader who is able to read only with an eye on possible theoretical interpretations of what he is reading--in short, for a literary scientist: such a ‘reflected’ approach in no way diminishes our enjoyment of the work--on the contrary, it supplements our reading with a surplus-enjoyment which is one of the trademarks of true modernism.”<br /><br /><br /><br />____________<br />Further precedence for <i>Finnegans Wake</i>.<br /><br />From Rabelais’ second book, <i>Gargantua</i>, chapter two, we find the following <i>Antidoted Bubbles</i>, apparently a satirical poem known as a <i>coq-à-l’âne</i> (“*” represents the bits of manuscript eaten by mice):<br /><br /><br />********arrived the Cimbrian conqueror,<br />*******in through air for fear of all that dew,<br />******e arrives, the tubs can take no more<br />****resh butter pouring down like stew.<br />**bespattered grandma in full view:<br />She cried aloud: ‘Herren. Fish him right out!<br />His beard cow-patted is as if by glue;<br />Or hold him a ladder, better ‘tis than nowt.’<br /><br />To lick his slipper some said was true bliss,<br />Better indeed than pardoners to pay;<br />But an affected rascal came amiss<br />Up from the dip where roaches swim and play,<br />And said, ‘My Lords, for God’s sake, your hands stay!<br />The eel is in that booth quite unrevealed.<br />There you shall find, if you would look that way,<br />Deep in his amice a great fault concealed.’<br /><br />He was about that Chapter to intone<br />But found, within, the horns of a young cow.<br />‘My mitre’s depth,’ he said, ‘is cold as stone.<br />It chills my freezing brain, I know not how.’<br />With turnips’ reek they warm his icy brow:<br />He’d stay at home quite happily and glad<br />If they should find new harnesses somehow<br />For all those folk whose brains have turned quite mad.<br /><br /><br />Eleven stanzas follow. Also, one things of Lewis Carroll and Dr Seuss in relation to the playfulness of <i>Finnegans Wake</i>.<br /><br /><br />___________<br />If you can stomach the insipid, cloying prose, Michael Chabon has a piece in the NYRB reflecting on a year of reading the <i>Wake</i>: <a href="http://www.nybooks.com/articles/archives/2012/jul/12/what-make-finnegans-wake/?pagination=false#fnr-2" rel="nofollow noopener">What to Make of Finnegans Wake?</a>. (Thank you, MJ, for the link).<br /><br />____________<br />Donald Barthelme, that master of postmodern minimalism, said "After all, we are all realists." How does that apply to Joyces <i>Wake</i>? The first hypothesis would be that it is a realism relating to the experience of language, night(marre)-time language, or, in accordance with the 21st being the century of the <i>Wake</i>, the experience of being in between language(s). Joyce lived between English and Gaelic. We are in a globalized world in which English appears as the contemporary monolithic Latin. But isn't Joyce's work suggesting an alternative experience against English-as-monolith? that what our multi-cultural, multi-lingual global experience implies is a betweenness? Languages have always existed as a betweenness of official, universalizable Sprache and local, particularistic Dialekt; witness Spanglish or Ebonics. Can we locate the language wars between prescriptivists and descriptivists in this same betweenness? that we should, rather than prescribe how Joyce should have written his book, take him as describing a possibility of our being between language(s)? between knowing the rules and at the same time knowing that (guilty!) we break them and how those rules are always the possibility of their own trespass?<br /><br /><br />A precedent for the <i>Wake</i> discovered in ancient India, a Sanskrit novella, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9035802.Subandhu_s_Vasavadatta" title="Subandhu's Vasavadatta by Subandhu" rel="nofollow noopener">Subandhu's Vasavadatta</a>, from the 5th or 6th century, a.d. Take the sentence from the <i>Wake</i>, "they were yung and easily freudened," and imagine translating it into a non-European language and culture. Compare the result with this passage, rendered by two translators, an American and an Indian:<br /><br />From Louis Gray (1913):<br />"Thus, even though a [Bhīma], he is [[no foe of Baka]], for he is [horrible] and a [[foe of them that praise him]]; though a [fire], he is a [[wind]], for he is a [devourer of his own place of refuge] and a [[dog in his mother]]". (Single brackets represent single puns, double double.)<br /><br />From Harinath De (1908, published 1994):<br />"The wicked man combines the incompatible appellations of being <i>Bhīma</i> and yet no foe to the demon <i>Baka</i> in the sense that he is terrible and hostile to those worthy of praise; of being <i>āśrayāśa</i> fire, yet <i>Matariśvā</i> in the sense of being a destroyer of shelters and adopting a canine behaviour towards his own mother." <br /><br />(Taken from Steven Moore's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7783692.The_Novel_An_Alternative_History_Beginnings_to_1600" title="The Novel An Alternative History Beginnings to 1600 by Steven Moore" rel="nofollow noopener">The Novel: An Alternate History volume 1: Beginnings to 1600</a>, pp424-425.)<br /><br />What does this suggest about the <i>Wake</i>? On the other hand, how is it possible for a reader to believe that any author could write a novel which contains nothing but univocal semantic content? Irony, metaphor, pun, ambiguity and ambivalence are the constituents, the conditions of possibility, of the novel. Isn't this what the <i>Wake</i> carries out to a degree of genius beyond the capacity of a merely single natural language?<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />_____________<br />Several artificial languages are woven into The Wake. Here's something more recent:<br />from The New Yorker:<br />"Utopian for Beginners: An amateur linguist loses control of the language he invented."<br />by Joshua Foer<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.newyorker.com/reporting/2012/12/24/121224fa_fact_foer#ixzz2FVhI1s19">http://www.newyorker.com/reporting/20...</a><br />[Of course, Wakian is in no way to be construed as 'artificial,' only 'artful.'<br /><br /><br /><br />___________________<br /><i>The Restored Finnegans Wake</i>. Eds. Danis Rose and John O'Hanlon. London: Penguin Classics, 2012.<br /><br />This is a clear reading text produced out of 30 years labor upon Joyce's various versions of and notebooks for his <i>Work in Progress/Finnegans Wake.</i> Forth coming is a hypertext edition of the <i>Wake</i> which will include all of the pre-<i>Wake</i> drafts as well as the entirety of the extant notebooks. This hypertext edition underlies the recently published restored <i>Wake</i>. For more information see the <a href="http://www.houyhnhnmpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Houyhnhnm Press </a>.<br /><br />My intention is to read one to two pages of the <i>Wake</i> per day. This oughta keep me reading for 491 days. I will also regularly push a status update in order to increase my status in the literary world as One Who Reads the <i>Wake</i>, or perhaps so that one may observe my failure to get past page 20, to have all that pretentiousness rubbed back in my own face. I do not intend to write a review. That would seem a bit obscene. But I have 491 days to decide about that. <br /><br />My reading, at the beginning is untethered, but, being bookish as I am, I will likely accumulate some kind of bookish reading partner, if anything insightful presents itself.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Annutter Review of mine of Wake, wherein is out=line’d our Wake wit chapter first-lines ::<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/858403260">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14008063754570682238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14589549543453864986" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1995/03/causabons-key-to-all-mythologies-with.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br />Causabon's Key To All Mythologies with Guinness and Opera: “Finnegans Wake” by James Joyce<br /><br /><br /><br />"We'll meet again, we'll part once more. The spot I'll seek if the hour you'll find. My chart shines high where the blue milk's upset."<br /><br />In “Finnegans Wake” by James Joyce<br /><br /><br />Joyce could really write. “Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man” is exquisite, and “Ulysses” is a masterpiece. I see Joyce as a product of his 'modernist' era, certainly, but a sincere one. He was reaching for something, a kind of synthesis of prose and poetry that came close to the true language of the mind. It's remarkable how much of Finnegans Wake is comprehensible, in spite of the fact that Joyce's words don't actually exist; we know what he means, or we can guess at it, which would be impossible if it was just gibberish. <br /><br /><br />There's more stuff on the other side of the rainbow.</span>
