<div id="review_25624027" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25624027" />
    <a title="Nick Black" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/655723-nick-black"><img alt="Nick Black" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1315018121p2/655723.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/25624027?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Black" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Black" href="/user/show/655723-nick-black">Nick Black</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140062861" href="/book/show/781824.Finnegans_Wake">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25624027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18083275917828957336">Major life admission: I've never actually finished this book. Let me explain.<br/><br/>I first came across Joyce in the spring of 1996. When "Araby" was assigned for an evening's BritLit homework, I was fifteen and still playing <i>Final Fantasy Legend</i> on my Gameboy from that Christmas ; up until that MARTA ride home, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5107.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye' title='The Catcher in the Rye by J.D. Salinger' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Catcher in the Rye</a> had seemed the most meaningful and personally evocative thing around. The last line almost blinded me:<br/><br/><b>Gazing up into the darkness I saw myself as a creature driven and de</b></span>
  <span id="freeText18083275917828957336" style="display:none">Major life admission: I've never actually finished this book. Let me explain.<br /><br />I first came across Joyce in the spring of 1996. When "Araby" was assigned for an evening's BritLit homework, I was fifteen and still playing <i>Final Fantasy Legend</i> on my Gameboy from that Christmas ; up until that MARTA ride home, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5107.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye by J.D. Salinger" rel="nofollow noopener">The Catcher in the Rye</a> had seemed the most meaningful and personally evocative thing around. The last line almost blinded me:<br /><br /><b>Gazing up into the darkness I saw myself as a creature driven and derided by vanity; and my eyes burned with anguish and anger.</b><br /><br /><i>what?</i> where did that come from? is that it? is this for real?<br /><br />I reread "Araby" several times before devouring the essay entitled "Epiphany" with which Prentice-Hall saw fit to prepare innocent 10th graders, fattened by a year of American Lit wasted on Transcendentalist saying-everything-about-nothing hogwash and subsequent semester devoted to memorizing large swaths of <i>The Exeter Book</i> and Chaucer's Tales in their original, etymologically-accurate forms (this being a Catholic school, we were spared significant exposure to the great heretic Milton). I made a decision there: I would have more of this Mr. Joyce. We would become friends.<br /><br />Scouring Ms. Via's shelf found an older Prentice-Hall and, more importantly, "Eveline", and this was <i>love</i> you must understand. These were hammer blows. Mr. Huelin had some months earlier delivered grand exhortations regarding Donne's "Batter my Heart"; oh, how he wanted us to grok one-hundredth of his grokking! (Mixed ninth- and tenth-graders are, of course, at best a uneconomical vein to mine for such ores (no matter how snappy their uniforms) and Mr. Huelin was finishing his Ph.D. at UChicago last I checked).<br /><br /><b>A bell clanged upon her heart. She felt him seize her hand:<br /><br />"Come!"<br /><br />All the seas of the world tumbled about her heart. He was drawing her into them: he would drown her. She gripped with both hands at the iron railing.<br /><br />"Come!"<br /><br />No! No! No! It was impossible. Her hands clutched the iron in frenzy. Amid the seas she sent a cry of anguish.<br /><br />"Eveline! Evvy!"<br /><br />He rushed beyond the barrier and called to her to follow. He was shouted at to go on but he still called to her. She set her white face to him, passive, like a helpless animal. Her eyes gave him no sign of love or farewell or recognition.</b><br /><br />Yes, through the ABBA rhyme scheme couldn't even in those days help but sound childish, and despite finding mention of God and Bog more repugnant at that time than before or after, I then understood Donne's need:<br /><br /><b>BATTER my heart, three person'd God; for, you	 <br />As yet but knocke, breathe, shine, and seeke to mend;	 <br />That I may rise, and stand, o'erthrow mee,'and bend	 <br />Your force, to breake, blowe, burn and make me new.</b><br /><br />I held that no literature's more broadly superb than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2158025.The_Odyssey_Translated_by_Robert_Fitzgerald" title="The Odyssey Translated by Robert Fitzgerald by Homer" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a> almost before I bought a copy, certainly well before finishing it (dazzled, overwhelmed, oftentimes giddy and in no small portion bewildered) several sublime weeks later. I knew immediately that, so long as my eyes and mind serviced and my soul drank deeply, I'd love and reread and take still more away from Joyce's <i>mattoncino</i>. Eleven years and ~8 readings of varying sedulousness have proven it, in the terms of Weiner, a positive feedback loop; in its shadow and those of a few others (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/57913.The_Illuminatus__Trilogy_The_Eye_in_the_Pyramid__The_Golden_Apple__Leviathan" title="The Illuminatus! Trilogy The Eye in the Pyramid, The Golden Apple, Leviathan by Robert Shea" rel="nofollow noopener">The Illuminatus Trilogy</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/168668.Catch_22" title="Catch-22 by Joseph Heller" rel="nofollow noopener">Catch-22</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4934.The_Brothers_Karamazov" title="The Brothers Karamazov by Fyodor Dostoevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Brothers Karamazov</a>, etc) were stroked the broad outlines of a weltanschauung. It was into trembling hands that the <i>Wake</i> was handed, now in the fall of 1997.<br /><br />I've tried numerous times, but I'll try no longer. I've opened it up to different places and taken off riverrun round Adam and Eve's, but never made it more than a few dozen pages -- and by then, I've skipped a bit. I've read whole pages out loud, wondering how best to pronounce hundred-letter words representing the Voice of Thunder, imagining Joyce in Trieste with his eye patch, laughing at Eliot and cackling to himself "this is What the Thunder Said, Bitch: <br /><br />Ullhodturdenweirmudgaardgringnirurdrmolnirfenrirluk<br />kilokkibaugimandodrrerinsurtkrinmgernrackinarockar!<br /><br />Maybe if you'd listen, you'd know. BTW I met a shantihshantihshantih once, too; I ate it with some kidneys and relish [0:]." I've read Campbell's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59478.A_Skeleton_Key_to_Finnegans_Wake_Unlocking_James_Joyce_s_Masterwork" title="A Skeleton Key to Finnegans Wake Unlocking James Joyce's Masterwork by Joseph Campbell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Skeleton Key to Finnegan's Wake</a> and several others and tried to read them alongside, to no avail. I've tried to read it in the manner of deconstructionism; I've tried to read it as a tone poem and interspersed pages with meditations on Ireland and rivers and Vico's theories of cyclical history; I've tried to read it on combinations of most major pharmaceuticals and some out-of-the way ones; I've tried to read it in the dark. I've given up on trying to remember what happened ten pages ago, and at times on the same page, and made no more progress. I've read every word in the book, and yet haven't read a goddamn word at all.<br /><br />Finally, I accepted it as a pretty good trick, and put it aside. Kertwang! You got us that time, James. You certainly got anyone who reads <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218349.The_Role_of_Thunder_in_Finnegans_Wake" title="The Role of Thunder in Finnegans Wake by Eric McLuhan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Role of Thunder in Finnegan's Wake</a> and <i>certainly</i> got anyone who spent $80 for that particular privilege, a bit of literary theory that seemed satire. You got me good, Mr. Joyce. <br /><br />Bladyughfoulmoecklenburgwhurawhorascortastrumpapor<br />nanennykocksapastippatappatupperstrippuckputtanach.<br /><br />Bababadalgharaghtakamminarronnkonnbronntonnerronntuonnthunn<br />trovarrhounawnskawntoohoohoordenenthurnukawwwgofuckyourself.<br /><br />[0:] Patrick Chisholm, that was for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18083275917828957336" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>



  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3335805972" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3335805972" />
    <a title="Tobias" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34694041-tobias"><img alt="Tobias" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573238082p2/34694041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3335805972?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tobias" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tobias" href="/user/show/34694041-tobias">Tobias</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199695157" href="/book/show/14828979-finnegans-wake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34694041-tobias?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3335805972" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11521003244709096791">I won't pretend that I have half a grasp of a quarter of what is actually going on in this bewildering book, but if you find yourself having a hard time nuzzling out the puzzles of The Wake, you can comfort yourself with the fact that no one around Joyce understood what he was doing either. Not Nora, not Pound, not Eliot, not Wells, not Stanislaus, not Lewis, not anyone. He was wasting his genius, his time, his eyesight; why didn't he just write a bestseller that the poor people could understand</span>
  <span id="freeText11521003244709096791" style="display:none">I won't pretend that I have half a grasp of a quarter of what is actually going on in this bewildering book, but if you find yourself having a hard time nuzzling out the puzzles of The Wake, you can comfort yourself with the fact that no one around Joyce understood what he was doing either. Not Nora, not Pound, not Eliot, not Wells, not Stanislaus, not Lewis, not anyone. He was wasting his genius, his time, his eyesight; why didn't he just write a bestseller that the poor people could understand? Pound barked that they make it new, but little did he know that even he had a limit for how new it could be, and he wrote to Joyce in despair that not even a new cure for the clap could make up for Joyce's thunderclaps, but yet Joyce went on to spend 17 years creating the longest night in literary history. Scheherazade needed a thousand and one to tell her tales, Joyce was content with the one. Having already written the longest day, what did you expect? Joyce, after all, liked a certain degree of symmetry and cyclicality. The Yin to complement the Yang.<br /><br />It is difficult, they say, it is incomprehensible, others say, it is impenetrable still others say, the last mad product of the madder hatter. I suppose it is true to some degree, but it depends on what kind of boots you strap on before you venture into Joyce's dreamscape. Should you expect yourself to walk on firm ground all along the long way, able to sort out its motifs, its characters, its engine of conception and organisation, you will sorely sink. Not that all of that isn't there; only it's presented in fluidity, in motion, in twists and turns of phrase. Don't assume that you can read The Wake awake; this is night-time after all. Nothing stays the same for very long. Neither do we.<br /><br />Some critic of world literature, whose name and credentials escape me, would have it that The Wake is the most democratic of all books with regards to language and nationality; by this, I suppose that he presumes (and I assume that he has read a bit about it and not in it) that The Wake is a simple patchwork of bits and bites of different languages near and far, dead and alive, presented in paratactical prose. Language metamorphosis and synthesis. Isn't that wonderful? A work of all and yet no particular language(s) but its own. Only this isn't exactly true, because The Wake is in English through and through, though Joyce once wrote that he was done with it. If you don't believe me, read it out loud, which, essentially, is the best you can do with this. It looks strange on the page, but the syntax is just recognisably English. For all the learning Joyce looted and loaded into his works, he wrote first and foremost for the ear. There are, of course, scraps of other languages in Joyce's dirtdump, but they all serve the purpose to emphasise that English was already a hybrid language.<br /><br />There is a staggering sense of sound and music in its prose which, more often than not, passes into poetry, and prose done right, as William Gass had it, is poetry; and if you can't enjoy the way Joyce could stretch language to do anything he wanted to do with it, then what the hell do you even read him for? It took years of reading, often with long stretches of not reading it. But when I did, I went into it with an almost childish naiveté and sense of wonder of what language is capable of, which might, in the end, not be the worst way of approaching this.<br /><br />We'll meet again. We'll part once more. Until then, I suppose, we'll follow the dictum of Joyce - to live and love and laugh and leave and let it all happen again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11521003244709096791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_570731832" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570731832" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570731832?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=traditional-fiction">traditional-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2019-shelf">2019-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=mindfuq">mindfuq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=abandoned">abandoned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570731832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16104103634296375504">This has got to be the best, most fantastic, wonderful book ever written to have absolutely freaking defeated me. Not only is the wordplay and freakishly brilliant alliteration such that I want to roll around in it like a dog in autumn leaves, but the language is also so dense and impenetrable I can BARELY get a sense of what the F*** is going on.<br/><br/>Is it brilliant? Yeah, I can see that much. I can also so see that it was specifically written to break modern literature scholars from their dependenc</span>
  <span id="freeText16104103634296375504" style="display:none">This has got to be the best, most fantastic, wonderful book ever written to have absolutely freaking defeated me. Not only is the wordplay and freakishly brilliant alliteration such that I want to roll around in it like a dog in autumn leaves, but the language is also so dense and impenetrable I can BARELY get a sense of what the F*** is going on.<br /><br />Is it brilliant? Yeah, I can see that much. I can also so see that it was specifically written to break modern literature scholars from their dependence on LSD and Heroin. Both used at the same time. And this is the "lite" version of the drug which is much more insidious because it is even MORE addictive and it happens to kill you in about thirty days after reading. It's a socially-transmitted Irish cancer. It's also a mudkiss written by a psychotic who throws readers into the abyss without a parachute. It was written by the Joker. You know, the one that just wanted to watch the world burn. <br /><br />It's murdercock English. It's being peed on by pearlypets. It's joking around like a hearse on fire. It's a nappywink.<br /><br /><br />Honestly, I would NOT have DNF'd this at the midpoint if it wasn't so freaking dense. Or if I were completely drunk in a room full of other Irish foks shouting out random lines from this monstrosity. Or if I joined a cult, bringing this book with me to counteract the crazy by a more potent kind of crazy.<br /><br />But I did none of these things. I was DEFEATED. <br /><br />But I do it gracefully. I admit I was beaten by this madman.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16104103634296375504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bJv0ZKAOKU+3z5PQjtjRkDPQOOwjtZCoCpu3zlSyIlNWvZ22G1DgJxDkJ7F5LdtP5Y/fJO04Hs9R5fF/mfHQyQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iny687un53SJO/S8NnRr6vAPgX+Nus2x3k/1rAoOtvOwWtMhAPkuHC4QQN3BgWE1JlBmt0M3Q9aFMbMdx01EaQ==')})
  }

To be fair, Ellmann does have some distinct advantages. For starters, there\'s his deep mastery of the Irish milieu--demonstrated not only in this volume but in his books on Yeats and Wilde. He\'s also an admirable stylist himself--graceful, witty, and happily unintimidated by his brilliant subjects. But in addition, Ellmann seems to have an uncanny grasp on Joyce\'s personality: his reverence for the Irishman\'s literary accomplishment is always balanced by a kind of bemused affection for his faults. Whether Joyce is putting the finishing touches on Ulysses, falling down drunk in the streets of Trieste, or talking dirty to his future wife via the postal service, Ellmann\'s account always shows us a genius and a human being--a daunting enough task for a fiction writer, let alone the poor, fact-fettered biographer. In the latter part of this curious masterwork, a certain Jacques Moran is deputized by anonymous authorities to search for the aforementioned Molloy. In the trilogy\'s second novel, Malone, who might or might not be Molloy himself, addresses us with his ruminations while in the act of dying. The third novel consists of the fragmented monologue - delivered, like the monologues of the previous novels, in a mournful rhetoric that possesses the utmost splendor and beauty - of what might or might not an armless and legless creature living in an urn outside an eating house. Taken together, these three novels represent the high-water mark of the literary movement we call Modernism. Within their linguistic terrain, where stories are taken up, broken off, and taken up again, where voices rise and crumble and are resurrected, we can discern the essential lineaments of our modern Imprisoned for some weeks in a wir<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1987496656015162284\" style=\"display:none\">Ezra Pound\'s The Pisan Cantos was written in 1945, while the poet was being held in an American military detention center near Pisa, Italy, as a result of his pro-Fascist wartime broadcasts to America on Radio Rome. Imprisoned for some weeks in a wire cage open to the elements, Pound suffered a nervous collapse from the physical and emotional strain. Out of the agony of his own inferno came the eleven cantos that became the sixth book of his modernist epic, The Cantos, themselves conceived as a Divine Comedy for our time. The Pisan Cantos were published in 1948 by New Directions and in the following year were awarded the Bollingen Prize for poetry by the Library of Congress. The honor came amid violent controversy, for the dark cloud of treason still hung over Pound, incarcerated in St. Elizabeths Hospital for the Criminally Insane. Yet there is no doubt that The Pisan Cantos displays some of his finest and most affecting writing, marking an elegaic turn to the personal while synthesizing the philosophical and economic political themes of his previous cantos. They are now being published for the first time as a separate paperback, in a fully annotated edition prepared by Richard Sieburth, who also contributes a thoroughgoing introduction, making Pound\'s master-work fully accessible to students and general readers.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1987496656015162284\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n No reference to the present day is intended or should be inferred.\n\nThe sizable cast of characters includes anarchists, balloonists, gamblers, corporate tycoons, drug enthusiasts, innocents and decadents, mathematicians, mad scientists, shamans, psychics, and stage magicians, spies, detectives, adventuresses, and hired guns. There are cameo appearances by Nikola Tesla, Bela Lugosi, and Groucho Marx.\n\nAs an era of certainty comes crashing down around their ears and an unpredictable future commences, these folks are mostly just trying to pursue their lives. Sometimes they manage to catch up; sometimes it\'s their lives that pursue them.\n\nMeanwhile, the author is up to his usual business. Characters stop what they\'re doing to sing what are for the most part stupid songs. Strange sexual practices take place. Obscure languages are spoken, not always idiomatically. Contrary-to-the-fact occurrences occur. If it is not the world, it is what the world might be with a minor adjustment or two. According to some, this is one of the main purposes of fiction.\n\nLet the reader decide, let the reader beware. Good luck.\n\n--Thomas Pynchon\n\nAbout the Author:Thomas Pynchon is the author of V., The Crying of Lot 49, Gravity\'s Rainbow, Slow Learner, a collection of short stories, Vineland and, most recently, Mason and Dixon.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5144.James_Joyce">About James Joyce</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5144.James_Joyce"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1517863935p3/5144.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5144.James_Joyce">James Joyce
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
6,467
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10699812005570313327">James Joyce, Irish novelist, noted for his experimental use of language in such works as <i>Ulysses</i> (1922) and <i>Finnegans Wake</i> (1939). Joyce's technical innovations in the art of the novel include an extensive use of interior monologue; he used a complex network of symbolic parallels drawn from the mythology, history, and literature, and created a unique language of invented words, puns, and allusions</span>
  <span id="freeText10699812005570313327" style="display:none">James Joyce, Irish novelist, noted for his experimental use of language in such works as <i>Ulysses</i> (1922) and <i>Finnegans Wake</i> (1939). Joyce's technical innovations in the art of the novel include an extensive use of interior monologue; he used a complex network of symbolic parallels drawn from the mythology, history, and literature, and created a unique language of invented words, puns, and allusions. <br /><br />James Joyce was born in Dublin, on February 2, 1882, the eldest of ten surviving siblings, two other died of typhoid. His father was John Stanislaus Joyce, an impoverished gentleman, who had failed in a distillery business and tried all kinds of other professions, including politics and tax collecting. Joyce's mother, Mary Jane Murray, was an accomplished pianist, whose life was dominated by the Roman Catholic Church. In spite of their poverty, the family struggled to maintain a solid middle-class facade.<br /><br />From the age of six Joyce, was educated by Jesuits at Clongowes Wood College, at Clane, and then at Belvedere College in Dublin (1893-97). In 1898 he entered the University College, Dublin. Joyce's first publication was an essay on Ibsen's play <i>When We Dead Awaken</i>. It appeared in the <i>Fortnightly Review</i> in 1900. At this time he also began writing lyric poems.<br /><br />After graduation in 1902 the twenty-year-old Joyce went to Paris, where he worked as a journalist, teacher and in other occupations under difficult financial conditions. He spent a year in France, returning when a telegram arrived saying his mother was dying. Not long after her death, Joyce was traveling again. He left Dublin in 1904 with Nora Barnacle, a chambermaid who he married in 1931. <br /><br />Joyce published <i>Dubliners</i> in 1914, <i>A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</i> in 1916, a play <i>Exiles</i> in 1918 and <i>Ulysses</i> in 1922. In 1907 Joyce had published a collection of poems, <i>Chamber Music</i>.<br /><br />At the outset of the First World War, Joyce moved with his family to Zürich. In Zürich Joyce started to develop the early chapters of Ulysses, which was first published in France because of censorship troubles in the Great Britain and the United States, where the book became legally available only in 1933. In March 1923 Joyce started in Paris his second major work, <i>Finnegans Wake</i>, suffering at the same time chronic eye troubles caused by glaucoma. The first segment of the novel appeared in Ford Madox Ford's transatlantic review in April 1924, as part of what Joyce called Work in Progress. The final version was published in 1939.<br /><br />Some critics considered the work a masterpiece, though many readers found it incomprehensible. After the fall of France in World War II, Joyce returned to Zürich, where he died on January 13, 1941, still disappointed with the reception of <i>Finnegans Wake</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10699812005570313327" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
